Checkpoint
What absurd analogy does the author use to refute Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih's position?
A) Comparing the Companions to two women married to one man.
B) Comparing the Companions to a prophet and an unbeliever's grave.
C) Comparing the Companions to a blind man and a seeing man.
D) Comparing the Companions to two different types of trees.

Key Takeaway
Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih's claim that the Companions are polytheists yet forgiven is logically impossible; the only consistent view is to affirm their uprightness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two contradictory beliefs about someone, like believing they are wrong yet still expecting reward for them? How does this lesson expose the inconsistency in your heart?
?
When you hear a hadith about forgiveness, do you ever use it as a license to judge others while assuming you are safe? What does this passage teach you about applying promises to those who are not the intended recipients?
?
The author calls Bakr's position 'ignorance' (jahala). Is there an area in your own understanding of Islam where you have accepted a contradiction because it feels comfortable? Will you now search for the consistency that this lesson demands?

Sects and Disagreements

The Sin of Insulting Companions and Refuting the Mu'tazilite Position
Nasafi condemns insulting Companions and critiques Mu'tazilite positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why insulting the Companions is a grave sin and how the Mu'tazilite position of suspending judgment about the Companions' conflicts is erroneous.

Core Idea
Holding the Companions in reverence is foundational; suspending judgment about them is a misguided innovation.

Nasafi begins by establishing a firm principle: those who speak ill of the Companions are themselves judged as misguided. He then identifies the specific errors of the Mu'tazilite leaders Amr ibn Ubayd, Wasil ibn Ata, Dirar, and others, who either suspended judgment or claimed one party was correct without specification. This lesson examines that initial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the strong condemnation at the start.
The passage sets a stark tone: insulting Companions leads to being 'judged upon as misguided'.
2
Identify the two Mu'tazilite positions
The passage mentions two distinct errors: the suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) of Amr and Wasil, and the claim of one correct and one mistaken by Dirar and others.
Both are refuted because they undermine the established uprightness (ʿadala) of all Companions.
3
Understand the Sunni argument
Nasafi asserts that the consensus (ijmaʿ) on the Companions' uprightness is not removed by their disagreements. Thus, we must hold allegiance to both parties individually.
This is the key distinction: respecting all Companions despite conflicts.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'هذا مع ما فيه من الفساد'
Nasafi introduces the idea that suspending judgment about Ali is 'extreme corruption' because the evidence of his correctness is apparent.
This sets up the next lesson's analogies.

فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تُزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر، وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: "لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم" ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له فلا شك لأحد من المسلمين في كفره - عصمنا الله. وكذا الكلام في قتال أهل الشام بصفين على هذا فإن عليا كان هو المحق المصيب والأمر فيه أظهر، فإن عليا كان ممن أدخله عمر في أهل الشورى، وكان ذلك من عمر شهادة أنه أحق بذلك ممن نازعه مع أن المنازعة حدثت بعد انعقاد إمامته وتقرر خلافته، وبيعة غيره وجدت بعد بيعته فلم تكن الثانية منعقدة، ثم لا ارتياب لأحد له من العلم حظ في تفاوت ما بين علي ومعاوية - رضي الله عنهما - في الفضل والعلم والشجاعة والفناء والسابقة في الإسلام، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان خطأ معاوية ظاهرًا إلا أنه فعل ما فعل أيضا عن تأويل فلم يصر به فاسقا على ما قررنا، ثم لا شك أن من حارب عليا من الصحابة ومن غيرهم على التأويل لم يصر به كافرًا ولا فاسقا؛ ولهذا قال علي: "إخواننا بغوا علينا" وقال لابن طلحة: "أنا وأبوك من أهل هذه الآية: {وَنَزَعْنَا مَا فِي صُدُورِهِم مِّنْ غِلٍّ إِخْوَانًا عَلَىٰ سُرُرٍ مُتَقَابِلِينَ}". ثم اختلف متكلمو أهل السنة والجماعة في تسمية من خالف عليا باغيًا، فمنهم من امتنع عن ذلك فلا يجوز إطلاق اسم الباغي على معاوية، ويقول: ليس هذا أخطأ في اجتهاده، ومنهم من يطلق ذلك الاسم ويستدل بقوله تعالى: {وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا} الآية، ويقول النبي عليه السلام لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" ويقول علي: "إخواننا بغوا علينا" غير أنهم يمتنعون عن...
So whoever extends his tongue in insulting them is himself insulted regarding his religion, judged upon as being in error and innovation – may Allah protect us from that. And by encompassing this summary, we recognize the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in suspending judgment regarding them and their saying: 'We do not know who is correct among them and who is mistaken.' And the error of Dirar and Muammar and Abu Hudhayl in their saying: 'We know that one of them is correct and the other mistaken.' And we hold allegiance to each of the two parties individually because their uprightness (ʿadala) has been established by consensus (ijmaʿ) and is not removed by disagreement. And this, together with the corruption it contains, for suspending judgment regarding Ali when the evidences of his correctness are apparent, is extreme corruption, since forming allegiance to one of the two persons individually while knowing that one of them does not deserve that is invalid, because it involves, according to them, allegiance to an enemy of Allah with certainty. Then we say: It is incumbent upon you by the analogy of your statement that if two women, one of whom is more beloved to you but you do not know her specifically, you would marry each one of them individually; and similarly, regarding a grave of a prophet and a grave of an unbeliever, if you do not know each one specifically, it would be incumbent upon you to affirm the prophethood of each one individually, which is unbelief (kufr). And by this also appears the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the leader of the Bakriyya, where he claimed regarding Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr that they were hypocrites and polytheists except that they are among the people of Paradise because of the saying of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ regarding the people of Badr: 'Perhaps Allah تعالى looked upon them and said: Do whatever you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And whoever judges these eminent ones to be hypocrites and polytheists, then makes the polytheist forgiven, there is no doubt among any Muslim about his disbelief – may Allah protect us. Likewise is the speech regarding the fighting of the people of Syria at Siffin upon this: Verily Ali was the correct and right, and the matter is more evident in it. For Ali was among those whom Umar included in the council of consultation (ahl al-Shura), and that was from Umar a testimony that he was more deserving of that than those who disputed with him, even though the dispute occurred after the establishment of his imamate and the confirmation of his caliphate, and the pledge to others occurred after his pledge, so the second was not concluded. Then there is no doubt for anyone who has a share of knowledge regarding the difference between Ali and Muawiya – may Allah be pleased with them – in virtue, knowledge, courage, devotion, and precedence in Islam. And since this is the case, the error of Muawiya is apparent, except that he did what he did also based on interpretation (ta’wil), so he did not become a transgressor (fasiq) by it according to what we have established. Then there is no doubt that whoever fought Ali from among the Companions and others based on interpretation did not thereby become an unbeliever (kafir) nor a transgressor (fasiq). That is why Ali said: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us' and said to Ibn Talha: 'I and your father are among the people of this verse: {And We shall remove from their hearts any rancor, (so they will be) brothers, on thrones facing each other}.' Then the theologians of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamaʿa differed regarding labeling those who opposed Ali as transgressors (baghi). Some refrained from that, so it is not permissible to apply the name 'baghi' to Muawiya, and they say: He only erred in his ijtihad. And some apply that name and use as evidence the saying of Allah تعالى: {And if two parties among the believers fight} the verse, and the Prophet's saying to Ammar: 'The rebellious party will kill you,' and Ali's saying: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us,' except that they refrain from...
Source pages p. 1177

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the Sunni position regarding the Companions who were involved in the early civil wars (fitan), particularly the Battle of the Camel and Siffin. The author, Nasafi, begins by condemning those who insult the Companions, stating that such people are themselves judged as misguided innovators. He then identifies the errors of specific Mu'tazilite figures: Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata, who suspended judgment claiming ignorance of who was right among the fighting parties; and Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl, who claimed that one party was right and the other wrong but without specification. Nasafi asserts the Sunni consensus on the uprightness (ʿadala) of all Companions, which is not negated by their disagreements. He then demolishes the Mu'tazilite position of suspending judgment about Ali, arguing that the evidence of his correctness is clear, and that withholding allegiance from him while knowing he is right leads to absurd consequences, like the hypothetical case of marrying two women without knowing which is beloved or affirming prophethood for an unknown grave. He also refutes Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, who bizarrely claimed that Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr were hypocrites and polytheists yet still would enter Paradise due to the hadith about the people of Badr. Nasafi flatly declares such a person an unbeliever. Shifting to the battle of Siffin, Nasafi argues that Ali was clearly the rightful caliph: Umar included him in the shura, his caliphate was legitimately established, and Muawiya's opposition after the fact was invalid. He then compares the virtues of Ali and Muawiya, affirming Ali's superiority, and concludes that Muawiya's error was apparent but excused because he acted on interpretation (ta’wil). Thus, Muawiya and those who fought Ali did not become transgressors (fasiq) or unbelievers (kafir). Nasafi supports this with Ali's own words calling them 'brothers' and citing a Quranic verse about removing rancor from hearts. Finally, he presents an internal Sunni disagreement: some theologians forbid labeling Muawiya as 'baghi' (rebellious) because he only erred in ijtihad, while others permit the term based on Quranic and hadith evidence that the opposing party in a fight between believers can be called 'baghi', but they still refrain from applying it to specific individuals. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear that the correct Sunni stance is to avoid takfir or tafsiq of any Companion based on these conflicts.

Key Terms
التوقف
العدالة
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence for someone who slanders the Companions?
A) They are still considered a believer but sinful.
B) They are judged as misguided and an innovator.
C) They are only rebuked lightly.
D) They are considered an unbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Insulting Companions is a grave sin, and the Mu'tazilite suspension of judgment is refuted by the consensus on their uprightness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about conflicts among the Companions, does your heart feel confused or distant from them? How does the truth that all of them are just and upright reshape your relationship with them? What specific habit can you adopt today to honour them in your speech, especially to guard against subtle criticism or indifference?
?
The Mu'tazilites claimed neutrality, but Nasafi calls it misguidance. In your own spiritual life, where have you excused yourself by saying 'I don't know who is right' to avoid taking a stand on a matter of truth? What would it cost you to commit to what has been clarified by the scholars, even if it means leaving the comfort of not deciding?
?
If you were to stand before Allah and He asked you about your attachment to the Companions, would you describe it as love or loyalty to all, or do you find yourself preferring some and criticizing others? What step can you take this week to deepen your love for them through learning their merits?

The Logical Absurdity of the Mu'tazilite Position and the Refutation of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih
Nasafi uses analogies and refutes Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses reductio ad absurdum to reject the Mu'tazilite stance and why Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih's claim is considered kufr.

Core Idea
Suspending judgment on Ali leads to impossible conclusions; and calling pious Companions polytheists is outright disbelief.

Nasafi now deploys two striking analogies to expose the absurdity of withholding allegiance from Ali. He then turns to refute the extreme Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, who bizarrely combined claims of hypocrisy and forgiveness. This lesson focuses on these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with analogies
First, read the passage that presents two analogies: the marriage of two unknown women and the grave of a prophet vs. an unbeliever.
These analogies demonstrate the absurd consequences of the Mu'tazilite position.
2
Understand the analogy's point
If you must treat two possibilities equally without knowing which is correct (as in the Mu'tazilite view), you would have to do contradictory actions like marrying both women or affirming prophethood for an unbeliever's grave.
This shows the position is logically untenable.
3
Examine the refutation of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih
Bakr claimed that Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr were hypocrites and polytheists yet still among the people of Paradise due to the Badr forgiveness hadith.
Nasafi declares this is clear kufr because it involves judging Companions as polytheists while claiming forgiveness.
4
Reflect on the severity
Note the supplication 'may Allah protect us' and the strong language 'no doubt among any Muslim about his disbelief'.
This underscores the seriousness of deviating on this issue.

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the Sunni position regarding the Companions who were involved in the early civil wars (fitan), particularly the Battle of the Camel and Siffin. The author, Nasafi, begins by condemning those who insult the Companions, stating that such people are themselves judged as misguided innovators. He then identifies the errors of specific Mu'tazilite figures: Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata, who suspended judgment claiming ignorance of who was right among the fighting parties; and Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl, who claimed that one party was right and the other wrong but without specification. Nasafi asserts the Sunni consensus on the uprightness (ʿadala) of all Companions, which is not negated by their disagreements. He then demolishes the Mu'tazilite position of suspending judgment about Ali, arguing that the evidence of his correctness is clear, and that withholding allegiance from him while knowing he is right leads to absurd consequences, like the hypothetical case of marrying two women without knowing which is beloved or affirming prophethood for an unknown grave. He also refutes Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, who bizarrely claimed that Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr were hypocrites and polytheists yet still would enter Paradise due to the hadith about the people of Badr. Nasafi flatly declares such a person an unbeliever. Shifting to the battle of Siffin, Nasafi argues that Ali was clearly the rightful caliph: Umar included him in the shura, his caliphate was legitimately established, and Muawiya's opposition after the fact was invalid. He then compares the virtues of Ali and Muawiya, affirming Ali's superiority, and concludes that Muawiya's error was apparent but excused because he acted on interpretation (ta’wil). Thus, Muawiya and those who fought Ali did not become transgressors (fasiq) or unbelievers (kafir). Nasafi supports this with Ali's own words calling them 'brothers' and citing a Quranic verse about removing rancor from hearts. Finally, he presents an internal Sunni disagreement: some theologians forbid labeling Muawiya as 'baghi' (rebellious) because he only erred in ijtihad, while others permit the term based on Quranic and hadith evidence that the opposing party in a fight between believers can be called 'baghi', but they still refrain from applying it to specific individuals. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear that the correct Sunni stance is to avoid takfir or tafsiq of any Companion based on these conflicts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Bakr's view is a valid theological opinion combining hadith and caution.
Correction: The passage teaches that claiming Companions are polytheists and hypocrites is unequivocally kufr, even if one attaches a condition of forgiveness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of someone who claims that Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr are hypocrites and polytheists but still forgiven?
A) He is mistaken but still a Muslim.
B) He is an unbeliever (kafir) with no doubt.
C) He is a misguided innovator.
D) He is correct because of the Badr hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position leads to absurdity, and Bakr's claim of hypocrisy mixed with forgiveness is clear kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever remained neutral or silent about a clear truth because it was uncomfortable or cost you something? How does Nasafi's analogy of the two women expose the hidden danger in your neutrality? What will you do differently starting today?
?
Bakr twisted a hadith to excuse his false opinion about the Companions. In your own life, do you ever use a verse or hadith to justify a sin or wrong belief you are unwilling to give up? How will you repent and seek the true understanding of that text?

Ali's Right and Muawiya's Error: The Role of Ijtihad and the 'Baghi' Label
Nasafi discusses Ali's righteousness, Muawiya's error by ijtihad, and the baghi label.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni reasoning for Ali's correctness in the Battle of Siffin, why Muawiya's error is excused by ijtihad, and the differing views on labeling him as 'baghi'.

Core Idea
Ali was unmistakably right, but Muawiya's error based on interpretation does not make him a sinner.

Nasafi now turns to the Battle of Siffin, presenting arguments for Ali's superior claim and Muawiya's error. He concludes that Muawiya did not become a transgressor because he acted on ijtihad. Finally, he explains the internal Sunni disagreement on whether to label Muawiya as a 'baghi' (rebel). This lesson covers these crucial points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Siffin
Read the section detailing Ali's inclusion in the shura and the comparison of virtues between Ali and Muawiya.
Nasafi highlights Ali's credentials from Umar's shura and his superior qualities.
2
Understand Muawiya's error and its excuse
Nasafi states that Muawiya was clearly in error, but his action was based on ta'wil (interpretation), so he did not become a fasiq.
This is a key Sunni principle: honest ijtihad, even if wrong, does not make one a sinner.
3
Examine the Sunni disagreement on labeling
Some Sunni scholars forbid calling Muawiya 'baghi', while others permit it using Quran and hadith evidence, but they still refrain from applying it to him.
Note the distinction: they cite evidence but do not condemn him specifically.
4
Reflect on Ali's own words
Ali called his opponents 'our brothers' and cited the verse about removing rancor, showing his magnanimity.
This reinforces the Sunni stance of not declaring takfir or tafsiq.

فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تُزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر، وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: "لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم" ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له فلا شك لأحد من المسلمين في كفره - عصمنا الله. وكذا الكلام في قتال أهل الشام بصفين على هذا فإن عليا كان هو المحق المصيب والأمر فيه أظهر، فإن عليا كان ممن أدخله عمر في أهل الشورى، وكان ذلك من عمر شهادة أنه أحق بذلك ممن نازعه مع أن المنازعة حدثت بعد انعقاد إمامته وتقرر خلافته، وبيعة غيره وجدت بعد بيعته فلم تكن الثانية منعقدة، ثم لا ارتياب لأحد له من العلم حظ في تفاوت ما بين علي ومعاوية - رضي الله عنهما - في الفضل والعلم والشجاعة والفناء والسابقة في الإسلام، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان خطأ معاوية ظاهرًا إلا أنه فعل ما فعل أيضا عن تأويل فلم يصر به فاسقا على ما قررنا، ثم لا شك أن من حارب عليا من الصحابة ومن غيرهم على التأويل لم يصر به كافرًا ولا فاسقا؛ ولهذا قال علي: "إخواننا بغوا علينا" وقال لابن طلحة: "أنا وأبوك من أهل هذه الآية: {وَنَزَعْنَا مَا فِي صُدُورِهِم مِّنْ غِلٍّ إِخْوَانًا عَلَىٰ سُرُرٍ مُتَقَابِلِينَ}". ثم اختلف متكلمو أهل السنة والجماعة في تسمية من خالف عليا باغيًا، فمنهم من امتنع عن ذلك فلا يجوز إطلاق اسم الباغي على معاوية، ويقول: ليس هذا أخطأ في اجتهاده، ومنهم من يطلق ذلك الاسم ويستدل بقوله تعالى: {وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا} الآية، ويقول النبي عليه السلام لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" ويقول علي: "إخواننا بغوا علينا" غير أنهم يمتنعون عن...
So whoever extends his tongue in insulting them is himself insulted regarding his religion, judged upon as being in error and innovation – may Allah protect us from that. And by encompassing this summary, we recognize the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in suspending judgment regarding them and their saying: 'We do not know who is correct among them and who is mistaken.' And the error of Dirar and Muammar and Abu Hudhayl in their saying: 'We know that one of them is correct and the other mistaken.' And we hold allegiance to each of the two parties individually because their uprightness (ʿadala) has been established by consensus (ijmaʿ) and is not removed by disagreement. And this, together with the corruption it contains, for suspending judgment regarding Ali when the evidences of his correctness are apparent, is extreme corruption, since forming allegiance to one of the two persons individually while knowing that one of them does not deserve that is invalid, because it involves, according to them, allegiance to an enemy of Allah with certainty. Then we say: It is incumbent upon you by the analogy of your statement that if two women, one of whom is more beloved to you but you do not know her specifically, you would marry each one of them individually; and similarly, regarding a grave of a prophet and a grave of an unbeliever, if you do not know each one specifically, it would be incumbent upon you to affirm the prophethood of each one individually, which is unbelief (kufr). And by this also appears the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the leader of the Bakriyya, where he claimed regarding Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr that they were hypocrites and polytheists except that they are among the people of Paradise because of the saying of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ regarding the people of Badr: 'Perhaps Allah تعالى looked upon them and said: Do whatever you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And whoever judges these eminent ones to be hypocrites and polytheists, then makes the polytheist forgiven, there is no doubt among any Muslim about his disbelief – may Allah protect us. Likewise is the speech regarding the fighting of the people of Syria at Siffin upon this: Verily Ali was the correct and right, and the matter is more evident in it. For Ali was among those whom Umar included in the council of consultation (ahl al-Shura), and that was from Umar a testimony that he was more deserving of that than those who disputed with him, even though the dispute occurred after the establishment of his imamate and the confirmation of his caliphate, and the pledge to others occurred after his pledge, so the second was not concluded. Then there is no doubt for anyone who has a share of knowledge regarding the difference between Ali and Muawiya – may Allah be pleased with them – in virtue, knowledge, courage, devotion, and precedence in Islam. And since this is the case, the error of Muawiya is apparent, except that he did what he did also based on interpretation (ta’wil), so he did not become a transgressor (fasiq) by it according to what we have established. Then there is no doubt that whoever fought Ali from among the Companions and others based on interpretation did not thereby become an unbeliever (kafir) nor a transgressor (fasiq). That is why Ali said: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us' and said to Ibn Talha: 'I and your father are among the people of this verse: {And We shall remove from their hearts any rancor, (so they will be) brothers, on thrones facing each other}.' Then the theologians of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamaʿa differed regarding labeling those who opposed Ali as transgressors (baghi). Some refrained from that, so it is not permissible to apply the name 'baghi' to Muawiya, and they say: He only erred in his ijtihad. And some apply that name and use as evidence the saying of Allah تعالى: {And if two parties among the believers fight} the verse, and the Prophet's saying to Ammar: 'The rebellious party will kill you,' and Ali's saying: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us,' except that they refrain from...
Source pages p. 1177

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the Sunni position regarding the Companions who were involved in the early civil wars (fitan), particularly the Battle of the Camel and Siffin. The author, Nasafi, begins by condemning those who insult the Companions, stating that such people are themselves judged as misguided innovators. He then identifies the errors of specific Mu'tazilite figures: Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata, who suspended judgment claiming ignorance of who was right among the fighting parties; and Dirar, Muammar, and Abu Hudhayl, who claimed that one party was right and the other wrong but without specification. Nasafi asserts the Sunni consensus on the uprightness (ʿadala) of all Companions, which is not negated by their disagreements. He then demolishes the Mu'tazilite position of suspending judgment about Ali, arguing that the evidence of his correctness is clear, and that withholding allegiance from him while knowing he is right leads to absurd consequences, like the hypothetical case of marrying two women without knowing which is beloved or affirming prophethood for an unknown grave. He also refutes Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, who bizarrely claimed that Ali, Talha, and al-Zubayr were hypocrites and polytheists yet still would enter Paradise due to the hadith about the people of Badr. Nasafi flatly declares such a person an unbeliever. Shifting to the battle of Siffin, Nasafi argues that Ali was clearly the rightful caliph: Umar included him in the shura, his caliphate was legitimately established, and Muawiya's opposition after the fact was invalid. He then compares the virtues of Ali and Muawiya, affirming Ali's superiority, and concludes that Muawiya's error was apparent but excused because he acted on interpretation (ta’wil). Thus, Muawiya and those who fought Ali did not become transgressors (fasiq) or unbelievers (kafir). Nasafi supports this with Ali's own words calling them 'brothers' and citing a Quranic verse about removing rancor from hearts. Finally, he presents an internal Sunni disagreement: some theologians forbid labeling Muawiya as 'baghi' (rebellious) because he only erred in ijtihad, while others permit the term based on Quranic and hadith evidence that the opposing party in a fight between believers can be called 'baghi', but they still refrain from applying it to specific individuals. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear that the correct Sunni stance is to avoid takfir or tafsiq of any Companion based on these conflicts.

أهل السنة والجماعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Muawiya and those who fought Ali are necessarily sinners or even disbelievers.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they acted on ijtihad and are excused; they do not become fasiq or kafir.

Checkpoint
What is the reason that Muawiya did not become a transgressor (fasiq) despite opposing Ali?
A) He repented before death.
B) He acted based on interpretation (ta'wil).
C) He was not a Companion.
D) He only fought defensive battles.

Key Takeaway
Muawiya's error was in opposing the rightful caliph, but his ijtihad excuses him; however, some Sunni scholars still label him as 'baghi'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are absolutely certain you are right, how do you treat those who oppose you? Do you give them the same excuse of ijtihad that Nasafi gives Muawiya, or do you condemn them? This lesson calls you to examine your heart: have you ever called someone a 'rebel' without considering they may have sincerely erred? What would it take for you to say, as Ali did, 'our brothers have rebelled against us' instead of 'our enemies'? Commit to one relationship where you will soften your judgment this week.
?
Ali said to Ibn Talha that they and their fathers will be together in Paradise without rancor. Can you confidently say that about someone with whom you have a deep disagreement? If not, what does that reveal about your attachment to being right? This lesson demands that you prioritize brotherhood over being proven correct. Identify a person you have distanced yourself from due to a disagreement and take the first step toward reconciliation before next Friday.
?
Nasafi stops short of labeling Muawiya a 'baghi' despite the evidence, because he fears overstepping. Is there a person or group you have already labelled and dismissed? This lesson confronts you with the risk of judging beyond your authority. Before you speak another word against them, reread Ali's words: 'I and your father are among the people of this verse: {And We shall remove from their hearts any rancor}.' Ask yourself: is my rancor preventing me from seeing them as brothers in the afterlife? What concrete action will you take to remove that rancor today?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate

Ali's Correctness and Muawiya's Error in Siffin
Imam Nasafi affirms Ali's correctness and Muawiya's error but not sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi holds that Ali was correct in Siffin while Muawiya erred, yet neither Muawiya nor his followers are considered sinners or disbelievers due to acting on interpretation (ta'wil).

Core Idea
It is possible to err in ijtihad without sinning.

This passage tackles a sensitive historical conflict: the Battle of Siffin between Ali and Muawiya. Imam Nasafi affirms Ali's correctness and Muawiya's error, but with an important nuance—he insists that error due to sincere interpretation does not entail sin or disbelief. As you read, pay attention to the evidence Nasafi marshals to support Ali's position and the way he shields Muawiya and his followers from theological condemnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the flow from a general statement about disbelief to the specific case of Siffin.
The passage begins with an emphatic 'no doubt'—that sets the tone.
2
Identify Nasafi's main claims
List the key points: (1) Ali was correct, (2) Muawiya erred, (3) Muawiya's error was due to ta'wil, (4) thus he is not a fasiq, (5) the same applies to all who fought Ali based on ta'wil.
Notice how Nasafi uses Umar's inclusion of Ali in the shura as a key proof.
3
Understand the role of ta'wil
Reflect on the concept of ta'wil (interpretation). Why does acting on interpretation excuse Muawiya from sin? What does this teach about judging historical figures?
This is a core principle in Sunni theology: error in ijtihad does not make one sinful.
4
Connect to Ali's own statement
Examine the quote from Ali: 'They are our brothers who have rebelled against us.' How does this reinforce Nasafi's argument? How does Ali's own attitude model the correct response?
Ali's words reflect the Quranic promise of removal of rancor (Surah al-Hijr:47).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا شك لأحد من المسلمين في كفره - عصمنا الله. وكذا الكلام في قتال أهل الشام بصفين على هذا فإن عليا الله كان هو المحق المصيب والأمر فيه أظهر، فإن عليا لله كان ممن أدخله عمر في أهل الشورى، وكان ذلك من عمر شهادة أنه أحق بذلك ممن نازعه مع أن المنازعة حدثت بعد انعقاد إمامته وتقرر خلافته، وبيعة غيره وجدت بعد بيعته فلم تكن الثانية منعقدة، ثم لا ارتياب لأحد له من العلم حظ في تفاوت ما بين علي ومعاوية - رضي الله عنهما - في الفضل والعلم والشجاعة والفناء والسابقة في الإسلام، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كان خطأ معاوية ظاهرًا إلا أنه فعل ما فعل أيضا عن تأويل فلم يصر به فاسقا على ما قررنا، ثم لا شك أن من حارب عليا له من الصحابة ومن غيرهم على التأويل لم يصر به كافرًا ولا فاسقا ؛ ولهذا قال علي - فهم إخواننا بغوا علينا - وقال لابن طلحة - أنا وأبوك من أهل هذه الآية: ﴿وَنَزَعْنَا مَا فِي صُدُورِهِم مِّنْ غِلٍّ إخواناً عَلَى سُرُرٍ مُتَقَبِلِينَ﴾
There is no doubt for any Muslim in his disbelief - may Allah protect us. And likewise the discussion regarding fighting the people of Syria at Siffin according to this: indeed Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) was the rightful and correct one, and the matter is more apparent. Indeed Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) was among those whom Umar included in the consultative council (ahl al-shura), and that was from Umar a testimony that he was more entitled to it than those who contested him, even though the contestation occurred after the consolidation of his imamate and the establishment of his caliphate, and the pledge of allegiance to others occurred after his pledge, so the second was not binding. Then there is no doubt for anyone having a portion of knowledge regarding the difference between Ali and Muawiya - may Allah be pleased with both - in merit, knowledge, bravery, self-sacrifice, and precedence in Islam. When the matter is thus, Muawiya's error was apparent, except that he did what he did also on account of interpretation (ta'wil), so he did not become a sinner (fasiq) by it according to what we have established. Then there is no doubt that whoever fought Ali from among the Companions and others on account of interpretation did not become a disbeliever (kafir) nor a sinner (fasiq) by it. For this reason Ali said: 'They are our brothers who have rebelled against us,' and he said to Ibn Talha: 'I and your father are among the people of this verse: {And We shall remove from their breasts any rancor; they will be brothers on couches facing each other}.
Source pages p. 1178

Commentary
This passage affirms the correctness of Ali's position in the Battle of Siffin and explains the status of Muawiya and those who fought Ali. The author states that no Muslim doubts the disbelief of a certain person (likely referring to an extreme sect), then parallels the discussion with Siffin. He argues that Ali was clearly in the right, as evidenced by Umar including him in the shura council, which is a testimony of his entitlement. The allegiance given to others after Ali's was invalid. The author then highlights the clear superiority of Ali over Muawiya in virtue, knowledge, bravery, sacrifice, and precedence in Islam, so Muawiya's error is obvious. However, Muawiya acted on interpretation (ta'wil), thus he did not become a sinner (fasiq). Similarly, any Companion or other who fought Ali due to interpretation did not become a disbeliever or sinner. The author supports this by quoting Ali's statement calling them 'brothers who have rebelled against us' and a verse from the Quran promising removal of rancor, implying that despite the conflict, they are ultimately brothers in faith.

Key Terms
تأويل
فاسق
بغى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any opposition to the rightful caliph automatically constitutes sin or disbelief.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if the opposition is based on sincere interpretation (ta'wil), the person errs but does not become a sinner or disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why did Muawiya not become a sinner (fasiq) for opposing Ali?
A) Because Muawiya was also a Companion and thus infallible.
B) Because Muawiya acted on interpretation (ta'wil) and ijtihad.
C) Because Muawiya repented before death.
D) Because the battle was not against Ali but against his followers.

Key Takeaway
In Sunni theology, error in ijtihad due to interpretation (ta'wil) does not render one a sinner or disbeliever, even when opposing the rightful imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim whose actions or beliefs seem clearly wrong to you, do you instantly label them as misguided or sinful? After this lesson, how might you reconsider your judgment, and what concrete change can you make in how you react to such disagreements?
?
Reflect on a time you were harsh with someone for a mistake they made. Could that person have had a sincere interpretation (ta'wil) that you dismissed? What does that say about your own humility, and what step can you take today to seek understanding before condemning?
?
Ali called his opponents 'brothers' despite their rebellion. Think of a person or group you consider spiritually or theologically far from you. Can you honestly call them 'brothers'? If not, what prevents you, and how can you begin to remove rancor from your heart as the verse promises (Quran 15:47)?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Disagreement on Labeling Muawiya a Rebel (Bāghī)
Nasafi presents two positions on calling Muawiya a rebel and defends Talha and Zubayr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions among Ahl al-Sunna mutakallimun regarding the application of the term 'baghi' to Muawiya and the rationale behind each position, as well as the defense of Talha and Zubayr's motives.

Core Idea
Not all rebellion is moral sin; ijtihad and context matter.

This lesson explores a nuanced theological disagreement within Ahl al-Sunna about labeling Muawiya and his supporters as 'rebels' (baghi). While some scholars avoided the term, citing ijtihad error, others applied it based on clear scriptural and prophetic evidence. The discussion reveals deeper principles about accountability and intent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's presentation of two distinct positions.
2
Identify the two positions
Look for the two groups: those who refrain from calling Muawiya a baghi and those who apply it. Note the evidence each side uses.
The first group says he erred in ijtihad; the second cites Quran 49:9, the hadith of Ammar, and Ali's statement.
3
Understand the Hanafi ruling on inheritance
The passage mentions that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ruled that a rebel does not lose inheritance if he kills his just heir. Reflect on how this connects to negating accountability (muaakhadha).
This shows that even if the term 'baghi' is applied, it does not imply moral sin that affects worldly rulings.
4
Examine the defense of Talha and Zubayr
Read the author's argument that Talha and Zubayr sought justice (retribution for Uthman), not power. Note how this refutes the Rafidite accusation of oppression.
The author emphasizes their reluctance to assume the caliphate and their eventual acceptance after consultation.
5
Synthesize the key takeaway
Consider how this lesson illustrates that labeling requires careful consideration of intentions and ijtihad, and that Ahl al-Sunna maintain a balanced view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلف متكلمو أهل السنة والجماعة في تسمية من خالف عليا باغيًا، فمنهم من امتنع عن ذلك فلا يجوز إطلاق اسم الباغي على معاوية، ويقول : ليس ذا أخطأ في اجتهاده، ومنهم من يطلق ذلك الاسم ويستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا﴾ الآية، ويقول النبي عليه السلام لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" ويقول علي : إخواننا بغوا علينا غير أنهم يمتنعون عن تسميتهم فساقًا لما مر، ولهذا ألحق أصحابنا رحمهم الله تأويلهم الفاسد بالصحيح في حق إبطال المؤاخذة، ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد – رحمهم الله -: إن الباغي إذا قتل مورثه العادل لا يحرم من الميراث كما لو قتل العادل مورثه. ومن امتنع عن إطلاق اسم الباغي على هؤلاء يؤول قوله عليه السلام: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية" فيقول: معناه الطائفة الطالبة دم عثمان، يقال: بغى إذا طلب. ولا يلتفت إلى إطلاق الروافض اسم الظالم والفاسق وغير ذلك، لما أقمنا من الدلالة على انتفاء هذه السمات عنهم. وكيف يجوز إطلاق هذه الأسامي على من كان ساعيًا في تحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل وطالبًا لما هو الواجب في ظاهر الشريعة من إيجاب القصاص غير ساع في الاستيلاء على الملك والترأس على الخلائق لينازع غيره ويزيل سلطانه إلى نفسه، ولهذا امتنع طلحة والزبير عن تقلد الإمامة والدخول في الخلافة بعد مقتل عثمان يأبى ذلك أشد الإباء إلى أن تبين له بمشورة أجلاء الصحابة وجه المصلحة في ذلك على ما مر ذكره، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم تكن تسميتهم بالظالم والفاسق جائزة مع ما بينا من ذلك، والله الموفق.
Then the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a differed regarding naming those who opposed Ali as rebels (baghi). Among them are those who refrained from that, so it is not permissible to apply the name 'baghi' to Muawiya, and they say: 'This one erred in his ijtihad.' Among them are those who apply that name and use as evidence the verse {And if two groups of believers fight} and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Ammar: 'The rebellious group (al-fiat al-baghiya) will kill you,' and Ali said: 'Our brothers have rebelled against us.' However, they refrain from calling them sinners (fasiq) for reasons mentioned. For this reason, our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) equated their corrupt interpretation with a correct one in terms of negating accountability. For this reason, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: 'If the rebel kills his just heir, he is not deprived of inheritance, just as if the just person kills his heir.' Those who refrain from applying the name 'baghi' to these people interpret the Prophet's statement 'the rebellious group will kill you' by saying: Its meaning is the group seeking the blood of Uthman; it is said: bagha when seeking. And no attention is paid to the Rafidites applying the name 'oppressor' and 'sinner' and so on, because we have established evidence that these descriptions are negated from them. How can these names be applied to those who were striving to achieve truth and abolish falsehood, and seeking what is obligatory according to the apparent aspect of the Sharia – establishing retribution – not striving to take over dominion and leadership over creation to dispute with others and remove his authority to himself? For this reason, Talha and Zubayr refrained from assuming the imamate and entering the caliphate after the killing of Uthman, refusing that most strongly until the aspect of benefit became clear to them through consultation with the senior Companions, as previously mentioned. When the matter is thus, calling them 'oppressor' and 'sinner' is not permissible, given what we have clarified. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1178

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna mutakallimun regarding the application of the term 'baghi' (rebel) to Muawiya and those who opposed Ali. Two positions are outlined: (1) Those who refrain, arguing that Muawiya merely erred in ijtihad, so the label 'baghi' is inappropriate. (2) Those who apply the term, citing Quran 49:9, the hadith about Ammar (that the rebellious group would kill him), and Ali's own statement calling them 'brothers who have rebelled.' However, both groups agree that they are not called sinners (fasiq). The author then explains that the Hanafi jurists (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) equated their corrupt interpretation with a correct one in terms of negating accountability, and they ruled that if a rebel kills a just heir, he does not lose inheritance rights—similar to a just person killing an heir (meaning accountability for sin is not assumed). Those who avoid the term 'baghi' interpret the hadith as referring to the group seeking vengeance for Uthman's blood, since the root 'bagha' can mean 'to seek.' The author then defends Talha, Zubayr, and others, arguing that their motives were not worldly power but justice—they sought retribution for Uthman's murder as required by the apparent meaning of Sharia. They only accepted the caliphate later after consultation. Therefore, calling them oppressors or sinners is impermissible. The passage concludes with 'Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
باغي
فئة باغية
تأويل
اجتهاد
مؤاخذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Applying the term 'baghi' to Muawiya necessarily implies that he was a sinner or wrongdoer in a moral sense.
Correction: The passage shows that even those who apply the term refrain from calling them sinners (fasiq), and the Hanafi ruling on inheritance indicates that accountability for sin is negated due to ijtihad.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for those who apply the term 'baghi' to Muawiya?
A) The consensus of the Companions.
B) The Quranic verse about fighting believers and the hadith about Ammar.
C) The fact that Muawiya declared war against the caliph.
D) The judgment of the first four caliphs.

Key Takeaway
The term 'baghi' can be applied technically without moral condemnation when ijtihad is involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about conflicts among the early Muslims, are you quick to label one side as wrong? How does the lesson's emphasis on ijtihad and intentions challenge you to withhold judgment? What would it mean for you to extend that same charity to Muslims you disagree with today?
?
The scholars refused to call Muawiya a sinner even while applying the term 'rebel'. Is there someone in your life whom you have reduced to a single label (e.g., 'sinner', 'hypocrite')? How can you separate a specific action from a person's overall moral standing, as the scholars did?
?
Talha and Zubayr refused power until they were sure of its benefit. Are there positions or authorities you cling to out of ego rather than sincerity? Will you examine your motives today and step back if needed, as they did?

Six Rules for Dealing with Rebels
Nasafi lists six rules for engaging rebels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six rules of engagement with rebels (Khawarij and ahl al-baghy) as outlined by Nasafi, emphasizing restraint and invitation before conflict.

Core Idea
Islam prescribes a measured, invitation-first approach towards rebels, not indiscriminate warfare.

Nasafi summarizes the sunnaic conduct towards rebels, derived from the practice of the early community. Before any conflict, rebels must be summoned and debated, and war is only defensive. This passage lists the specific obligations that protect against unnecessary bloodshed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very compressed style, listing rules in sequence.
2
Identify the six rules
The passage enumerates six distinct obligations. List them in order: summoning, debating, not initiating, issuing declaration, not pursuing fleeing, not killing wounded, not harming women.
The rules are separated by 'واو' (and) in Arabic; count them carefully.
3
Understand the rationale
For each rule, ask: why does the Sharia impose this restriction? Refer to the commentary to see how each rule reflects mercy and justice.
The goal is to minimize bloodshed and ensure the rebels have a chance to return to the truth.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that Islam permits indiscriminate warfare against rebels. How does this passage correct that?
This shows the balanced approach of Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لولا ما ذكر أحوالهم من الوقوف على ما هو الواجب في معاملة الخوارج، ومن يبتلى بمحاربته من أهل البغي من الابتداء باستدعائهم ومناظرتهم وترك مبادأتهم، والنبذ إليهم قبل نصب الحرب معهم، والامتناع عن محاربتهم إلى أن يبتدعوا، وترك اتباع مدبرهم، وتدفيف جريحهم، والتعرض لنسوتهم.
Then we say: If not for what we have mentioned of their conditions, regarding knowing what is obligatory in dealing with the Khawarij and whoever is afflicted by fighting them from the people of rebellion (ahl al-baghy): to begin by summoning them and debating them, and refraining from initiating (fighting) against them, and throwing (a declaration) to them before erecting war against them, and refraining from fighting them until they initiate, and refraining from pursuing their fleeing ones, and finishing off their wounded, and approaching their women.
Source pages p. 1179

Commentary
This passage summarizes the obligatory conduct towards rebels, specifically the Khawarij and similar groups (ahl al-baghy). The author implies that this conduct is derived from the precedents set by the early community. The rules are enumerated: (1) Begin by summoning them (to the truth) and debating them. (2) Do not initiate fighting; instead, wait for them to begin. (3) Issue a declaration (or ultimatum) before war. (4) Do not pursue those who flee. (5) Do not kill the wounded (i.e., do not finish them off). (6) Do not approach (i.e., harm) their women. These rules reflect the sunnaic approach of avoiding unnecessary bloodshed and ensuring that conflict is only defensive and limited.

Misconception: That Islam permits indiscriminate warfare against rebels without prior invitation or restraint.
Correction: This passage shows that even rebels are entitled to a formal invitation, debate, and multiple restraints during conflict.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first step in dealing with rebels?
A) Immediately engaging in battle.
B) Summoning and debating them.
C) Killing their wounded.
D) Pursuing them until they are all dead.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunna maintain that even rebels are entitled to invitation and restraint, limiting warfare to defensive measures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ruling on Labeling the Ahl al-Baghy as Fasiq or Zalim
The author argues against labeling rebels as fasiq or zalim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that those who fought against Ali (RA) cannot be labeled as fasiq or zalim, based on their intentions and interpretations.

Core Idea
Not all who rebel are unjust; intent and interpretation matter.

This passage tackles a sensitive historical issue: the status of those who opposed Ali (RA) in the Battle of the Camel and Siffin. The author defends them against labels like fasiq and zalim, arguing that their actions were based on sincere interpretation and not personal ambition. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author builds his argument using legal rulings and hadith.
2
Identify the main arguments
Look for the key points: Abu Hanifa's ruling on inheritance, the interpretation of the hadith about Ammar, and the dismissal of Rafidah's labels.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that Talha and Zubair were seeking truth and were reluctant to assume power, which shows their sincerity. This is why labeling them as fasiq or zalim is impermissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تسميتهم فساقًا لما مر، ولهذا ألحق أصحابنا رحمهم الله تأويلهم الفاسد بالصحيح في حق إبطال المؤاخذة، ولهذا قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد – رحمهم الله -: إن الباغي إذا قتل مورثه العادل لا يحرم من الميراث كما لو قتل العادل مورثه ومن امتنع عن إطلاق اسم الباغي على هؤلاء يؤول قوله عليه السلام الباغي لعمار: "تقتلك الفئة الباغية. فيقول: معناه الطائفة الطالبة دم عثمان، يقال: بغى إذا طلب، ولا يلتفت إلى إطلاق الروافض اسم الظالم والفاسق وغير ذلك، لما أقمنا من الدلالة على انتفاء هذه السمات عنهم، وكيف يجوز إطلاق هذه الأسامي على من كان ساعيًا في تحقيق الحق وإبطال الباطل وطالبًا لما هو الواجب في ظاهر الشريعة من إيجاب القصاص غير ساع في الاستيلاء على الملك والترأس على الخلائق لينازع غيره ويزيل سلطانه إلى نفسه، ولهذا امتنع طلحة والزبير عن تقلد الإمامة والدخول في الخلافة بعد مقتل عثمان رضي الله عنه يأبى ذلك أشد الإباء إلى أن تبين له بمشورة أجلاء الصحابة وجه المصلحة في ذلك على ما مر ذكره، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم تكن تسميتهم بالظالم والفاسق جائزة مع ما بينا من ذلك، والله الموفق.
Their labeling as fasiqun due to what passed, and for this our companions (rahimahumullah) attached their corrupted interpretation to the correct in regard to nullifying liability, and for this Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (rahimahuma Allah) said: Indeed the baghi (rebel) when he kills his just inheritor is not deprived of inheritance just as when the just person kills his inheritor. And whoever refrains from applying the name baghi to these (people) interprets his saying (peace be upon him) to Ammar: 'The baghiyah faction will kill you.' He says: Its meaning is the faction seeking the blood of Uthman; it is said: bagha when he seeks. And no attention is paid to the Rafidah's application of the name zalim and fasiq and other than that, because we have established evidence of the absence of these characteristics from them. And how is it permissible to apply these names to one who was striving to establish truth and abolish falsehood, and seeking what is obligatory in the apparent Sharia of imposing retaliation, not seeking to seize dominion and take leadership over creatures to dispute with others and transfer his authority to himself? And for this reason Talha and al-Zubair refrained from assuming the imamate and entering the caliphate after the killing of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) - he refused that most strongly until the aspect of maslaha in that was made clear to him through consultation of the senior companions, as mentioned. And if the matter is thus, their labeling as zalim and fasiq is not permissible, along with what we have clarified of that. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 1179

Commentary
This passage is a defense against labeling those who fought against 'Ali (RA) as fasiq or zalim. The author begins by referencing a previous point, then cites the opinion of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that a rebel (baghi) who kills a just person does not lose inheritance rights, similar to a just person killing an inheritor. Then discusses the hadith of Ammar ibn Yasir: 'The baghiyah faction will kill you.' The author clarifies that 'baghiyah' means those seeking (the blood of Uthman), not unjust oppressors. He dismisses the Rafidah's use of terms like zalim and fasiq as baseless. He argues that these companions (Talha, Zubair) were seeking truth and justice, not personal power, as evidenced by their reluctance to take the caliphate after Uthman's murder. Therefore, it is impermissible to apply these negative labels to them.

Rules of Engagement with Rebels
Enumeration of rules for engagement with rebels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the step-by-step method of dealing with rebels (ahl al-baghy) as derived from the practice of Ali (RA), emphasizing dialogue and restraint before conflict.

Core Idea
Dialogue before warfare: The Islamic method for dealing with rebels.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi outlines the ethical and legal framework for engaging with those who rebel against legitimate authority. Before any military action, the ruler must summon them for dialogue, issue warnings, and refrain from aggression until the rebels themselves initiate hostilities. These rules, rooted in the conduct of Ali (RA), ensure that justice is sought even in times of conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a single sentence listing multiple obligations.
The passage uses repetitive 'and' (و) to connect items.
2
Identify the phases of engagement
The rules can be grouped into pre-war steps (summoning, debating, warning) and during-war restrictions (not pursuing, not killing wounded, protecting women and property).
Each rule is a distinct obligation.
3
Understand the rationale
These rules aim to minimize bloodshed and uphold justice even against those who rebel. They are derived from the Sunnah of Ali (RA).
The passage explicitly credits 'Ali (RA) as the source.
4
Reflect on the modern relevance
Consider how these principles compare to modern rules of engagement in armed conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: لولا ما ذكر أحوالهم من الوقوف على ما هو الواجب في معاملة الخوارج ومن يبتلى بمحاربته من أهل البغي من الابتداء باستدعائهم ومناظرتهم وترك مبادأتهم والنبذ إليهم قبل نصب الحرب معهم والامتناع عن محاربتهم إلى أن يبتدعوا وترك اتباع مدبرهم وتدفيف جريحهم والتعرض لنسوتهم وترك اغتنام أموالهم وغير ذلك من الأحكام التي أخذها فقهاء الأمة عن معاملة عليه وسيرته فيهم.
Then we say: Were it not for what was mentioned of their states - standing upon what is obligatory in dealing with the Khawarij and whoever one is afflicted by fighting him from the people of baghy: from beginning by summoning them and debating them, leaving off initiating (fighting) them, and casting (the declaration) to them before setting up war with them, and refraining from fighting them until they begin (fighting), and leaving off pursuing their fugitive, and finishing off their wounded, and approaching their women, and leaving off seizing their property, and other than that of the rulings which the jurists of the Ummah took from the dealing of 'Ali (RA) and his conduct with them.
Source pages p. 1179

Commentary
This passage enumerates the rules of engagement with rebels (ahl al-baghy) based on the practice of 'Ali (RA). The author begins by saying that were it not for the need to know these rulings, they would not discuss their states. Then he lists the steps: first summon them to dialogue, do not initiate hostilities, give them notice before war, do not fight until they start, do not pursue those who flee, do not kill the wounded, do not harm women, do not take their property. These rulings are derived from Ali's treatment of the Khawarij and other rebels.

Abu Hanifa on Silence Regarding Fitan
Abu Hanifa's statement on preferring silence and the exception of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hanifa's principle that refraining from discussing the fitan among the Companions is safer in religion, and why it was still necessary to record their states.

Core Idea
Silence about Companions' conflicts is safer, but necessity demands knowledge.

This passage begins with a powerful statement from Abu Hanifa acknowledging that without Ali, we would not know how to deal with the Khawarij. He then asserts that remaining silent about their conflicts is safer in religion. However, he explains that a compelling need forced the scholars to record these events to protect hearts from false beliefs. This lesson explores the tension between these two commitments.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hanifa's statement
Read the first part of the passage where Abu Hanifa says 'Were it not for Ali, we would not know the sira...' and his preference for silence.
Notice the hadith he quotes: 'When my Companions are mentioned, keep silent.'
2
Identify the primary principle
Identify that Abu Hanifa clearly states that refraining from discussing the states of the Companions would be safer in religion and more in line with the Prophet's command.
3
Note the exception
Observe that the passage then introduces an exception: 'But the situation we described compelled us...' This shows that practical necessity overrides the general principle.
This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى قال أبو حنيفة – قدس الله روحه -: "لولا علي ما كنا نعرف السيرة في الخوارج لكان الكف عن ذكر أحوالهم والإغضاء عما جرى بينهم ودفع أحوالهم جملةً عن القلوب والألسن أسلم في الدين وأوفق بما مَنَّ الله علينا في تأخير انتسابنا عن الوقف الذي فيه خوف الاشتراك فيما لا يحل في الدين، والميل إلى ما الحق في غيره، وأقرب إلى ما دل عليه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بقوله: "إذا ذكر أصحابي فأمسكوا". إلا أن الحالة التي بيناها دعتنا إلى ذكر أحوالهم والإخبار عما جرى بينهم، فقام حفاظ الأمة بذكرها والمتكلمون بالكشف عن وجوه ذلك، صيانةً للقلوب عن اعتقاد ما لا يحل فيهم مع ما أكرمهم الله تعالى به من صحبة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ونصرة دينه، ونقل شريعته، وتبليغ وحيه، ثم إن فقهاء الأمة أخذوا ذلك عن معاملة علي الله، ثم يجوز أن يكون ذلك قد عرف النبي عليه السلام غيره لكنهم لم يخرجوا إلى وقت علي، ورأوا بالذي فعله هو الله كفاية إذ لم يغيروا عليه، وكان فعله ظاهراً بحيث عرفه الكل، وبلغ مبلغا لو ظهر منه منكر ليلزم التغيير، ويجوز أن يكون خصه النبي عليه السلام بتعليم الأحكام بما قد علم أنه يخص بالحاجة إلى ذلك، وفي قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "إِنَّا نقاتل على التنزيل وأنت تقاتل على التأويل" دليل أن جميع ما فعل فعل عن علم وعلى موافقة الشريعة، والله أعلم.
Until Abu Hanifa (may Allah sanctify his spirit) said: 'Were it not for 'Ali, we would not have known the conduct (sira) regarding the Khawarij. Refraining from mentioning their states and overlooking what occurred between them and removing their states entirely from hearts and tongues would be safer in religion and more appropriate to what Allah has bestowed upon us in delaying our lineage from the standing (al-waqf) which contains fear of sharing in what is not permissible in religion, and inclining to what is right in other than it, and closer to what the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) indicated by his saying: "When my Companions are mentioned, keep silent."' But the situation we described compelled us to mention their states and inform about what occurred between them. So the preservers (huffaz) of the Ummah stood to mention them and the mutakallimun (speculative theologians) to unveil the aspects of that, as a protection for hearts from believing what is not permissible concerning them, along with the honor that Allah the Exalted gave them of companionship of the Prophet (peace be upon him), supporting His religion, conveying His Sharia, and delivering His revelation. Then the jurists of the Ummah took that from the dealing of 'Ali (RA). Then it is possible that the Prophet (peace be upon him) had taught others that, but they did not emerge until the time of 'Ali, and they saw that what he did was sufficient (kifaya) since they did not object to him, and his action was apparent such that everyone knew it, and it reached a level that if any objectionable thing appeared from him, change (tahweel) would have been obligatory. And it is possible that the Prophet (peace be upon him) singled him out for teaching of rulings because he knew that he would be singled out for the need for that. And in his saying (peace be upon him): 'Indeed we fight on the revelation (tanzil) and you fight on the interpretation (ta'wil)' is evidence that all that he did was done with knowledge and in accordance with the Sharia. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 1179

Commentary
This passage contains a statement from Abu Hanifa acknowledging the importance of Ali's conduct in knowing how to deal with the Khawarij, and then a discussion about whether it is better to remain silent about the fitan among the Companions or to record it for the sake of protecting correct belief. The author argues that despite the preference for silence, the necessity of guarding hearts from false beliefs compelled the ulama to record and explain these events. He then provides evidence from the hadith about Ali fighting on interpretation as proof that all of Ali's actions were correct.

Key Terms
سيرة
خوارج
الوقف
أمسكوا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think discussing the fitan of the Companions is always forbidden.
Correction: The passage shows that while silence is preferred, necessity may require recording to protect correct beliefs.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa in this passage, what would be safer in religion?
A) Learning from Ali's conduct.
B) Refraining from discussing the states of the Companions.
C) Following the Khawarij.
D) Writing down all events.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa teaches that silence about the fitan among the Companions is generally safer, but necessity can override this principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself tempted to gossip about the conflicts of early Muslims, are you truly seeking religious benefit, or are you feeding your own curiosity? What would Abu Hanifa say to you in that moment, and will you obey?
?
You believe in 'silence is safer' for yourself, but when a friend brings up a controversial topic about the Companions, do you have the courage to remind them of the Prophet’s command, or do you stay silent out of social comfort?
?
Allah has blessed you by not placing you in the generation of fitan—yet you still indulge in discussing them. How many times will you read this hadith and walk away unchanged? What concrete step will you take today to guard your tongue and heart?

The Necessity to Record and the Proof of Ali's Correctness
Explanation of necessity for recording and proof from hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ulama recorded the events of fitan and how the hadith about Ali fighting on ta'wil proves his actions were correct.

Core Idea
Ulama recorded fitan to protect hearts, and the Prophet's hadith confirms Ali's correctness.

In the second part of this passage, the author explains the compelling reason that forced the scholars to record the events of the fitan despite the general preference for silence. He then provides a powerful proof from the Prophet's hadith that Ali's actions were in accordance with knowledge and Sharia. This lesson breaks down the roles of the huffaz, mutakallimun, and fuqaha, and the significance of the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part
Read from 'But the situation we described compelled us...' to the end of the passage.
Pay attention to the roles mentioned: huffaz, mutakallimun, fuqaha.
2
Identify the three groups
List the groups: preservers (huffaz), speculative theologians (mutakallimun), and jurists (fuqaha). Understand their roles in preserving and explaining the fitan.
The huffaz transmitted the reports, the mutakallimun explained them.
3
Understand the proof from hadith
The Prophet's saying 'We fight on tanzil and you fight on ta'wil' is presented as evidence that Ali acted with knowledge.
Tanzil means revelation, ta'wil means interpretation. This hadith shows Ali's fight was based on correct interpretation.
4
Note the possibilities
The passage also mentions two possibilities: others knew but waited for Ali's time, and that the Prophet specially taught Ali due to future need.
These support the idea that Ali's actions were divinely sanctioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى قال أبو حنيفة – قدس الله روحه -: "لولا علي ما كنا نعرف السيرة في الخوارج لكان الكف عن ذكر أحوالهم والإغضاء عما جرى بينهم ودفع أحوالهم جملةً عن القلوب والألسن أسلم في الدين وأوفق بما مَنَّ الله علينا في تأخير انتسابنا عن الوقف الذي فيه خوف الاشتراك فيما لا يحل في الدين، والميل إلى ما الحق في غيره، وأقرب إلى ما دل عليه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بقوله: "إذا ذكر أصحابي فأمسكوا". إلا أن الحالة التي بيناها دعتنا إلى ذكر أحوالهم والإخبار عما جرى بينهم، فقام حفاظ الأمة بذكرها والمتكلمون بالكشف عن وجوه ذلك، صيانةً للقلوب عن اعتقاد ما لا يحل فيهم مع ما أكرمهم الله تعالى به من صحبة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ونصرة دينه، ونقل شريعته، وتبليغ وحيه، ثم إن فقهاء الأمة أخذوا ذلك عن معاملة علي الله، ثم يجوز أن يكون ذلك قد عرف النبي عليه السلام غيره لكنهم لم يخرجوا إلى وقت علي، ورأوا بالذي فعله هو الله كفاية إذ لم يغيروا عليه، وكان فعله ظاهراً بحيث عرفه الكل، وبلغ مبلغا لو ظهر منه منكر ليلزم التغيير، ويجوز أن يكون خصه النبي عليه السلام بتعليم الأحكام بما قد علم أنه يخص بالحاجة إلى ذلك، وفي قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "إِنَّا نقاتل على التنزيل وأنت تقاتل على التأويل" دليل أن جميع ما فعل فعل عن علم وعلى موافقة الشريعة، والله أعلم.
Until Abu Hanifa (may Allah sanctify his spirit) said: 'Were it not for 'Ali, we would not have known the conduct (sira) regarding the Khawarij. Refraining from mentioning their states and overlooking what occurred between them and removing their states entirely from hearts and tongues would be safer in religion and more appropriate to what Allah has bestowed upon us in delaying our lineage from the standing (al-waqf) which contains fear of sharing in what is not permissible in religion, and inclining to what is right in other than it, and closer to what the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) indicated by his saying: "When my Companions are mentioned, keep silent."' But the situation we described compelled us to mention their states and inform about what occurred between them. So the preservers (huffaz) of the Ummah stood to mention them and the mutakallimun (speculative theologians) to unveil the aspects of that, as a protection for hearts from believing what is not permissible concerning them, along with the honor that Allah the Exalted gave them of companionship of the Prophet (peace be upon him), supporting His religion, conveying His Sharia, and delivering His revelation. Then the jurists of the Ummah took that from the dealing of 'Ali (RA). Then it is possible that the Prophet (peace be upon him) had taught others that, but they did not emerge until the time of 'Ali, and they saw that what he did was sufficient (kifaya) since they did not object to him, and his action was apparent such that everyone knew it, and it reached a level that if any objectionable thing appeared from him, change (tahweel) would have been obligatory. And it is possible that the Prophet (peace be upon him) singled him out for teaching of rulings because he knew that he would be singled out for the need for that. And in his saying (peace be upon him): 'Indeed we fight on the revelation (tanzil) and you fight on the interpretation (ta'wil)' is evidence that all that he did was done with knowledge and in accordance with the Sharia. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 1179

Commentary
This passage contains a statement from Abu Hanifa acknowledging the importance of Ali's conduct in knowing how to deal with the Khawarij, and then a discussion about whether it is better to remain silent about the fitan among the Companions or to record it for the sake of protecting correct belief. The author argues that despite the preference for silence, the necessity of guarding hearts from false beliefs compelled the ulama to record and explain these events. He then provides evidence from the hadith about Ali fighting on interpretation as proof that all of Ali's actions were correct.

Key Terms
حفاظ
متكلمون
فقهاء
تنزيل
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ultimate proof that Ali's actions were correct according to this passage?
A) The consensus of the Companions.
B) The hadith about fighting on tanzil and ta'wil.
C) The fact that no one objected.
D) Ali's own statements.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's hadith that Ali fights on ta'wil is the ultimate proof that his actions were in full agreement with Sharia.
